My only complaint, which another reviewer says as well, is that all of the clothing photographed in the book are pricey designer pieces, and when Lauren lists her favorite brands of jeans, for exaple, or places to shop, they're all up there too. It would be nice to include some more affordable suggestions or a section on how/where to shop for basics on a budget. Otherwise, this is a fun read with some good tips, and you'll enjoy it if you like Lauren's style.

0756404738|4.0|Wise Man's Fear picks up right where its predecessor left off, with the grown-up Kvothe, in hiding for reasons that haven't yet been revealed, recounting further chapters of the story of his younger self.    That story also picks up where it left off. If Rothfuss's first novel, The Name of the Wind, was a coming-of-age tale set in a fantasy world, well, this novel is one, too. Kvothe, the gifted, but hot-tempered young protagonist, continues his education, first as an arcanist at the University, then as an aide to a powerful noble, then training with a warrior people who have their own challenging spiritual/martial philosophy. Being Kvothe, he does nothing halfway, making new friends, new enemies, and people who are a bit of both. There's even a section where he learns the art of lovemaking in a typically larger-than-life way.    The rewards (and frustrations) of this novel probably won't surprise anyone who's finished the first book. As before, Rothfuss does a fine job developing his characters, lending their world both a firm seriousness and plenty of touches of humor. As before, he takes his sweet time with the story, devoting many pages to even relatively minor events in Kvothe's life. Fortunately, it's hard not to like the protagonist or relate to his journey, even though he's more than a bit full of himself (then again, this is what usually gets him into trouble).    However, these indulgences on the part of the author mean that the book can't help but feel a bit overstuffed. By the end, Rothfuss has gotten through a number of episodes in Kvothe's backstory, yet how they relate to the present-day story or why they matter... we still don't know. The author is asking for a leap of faith -- can he deliver on the other side? Also, I'm probably not the only reader who's grown impatient with Kvothe and Denna's relationship: unresolved sexual tension can only be sustained for so long before the writer runs out of excuses, and what was charming simply becomes irritating.    All in all, though, even if Rothfuss didn't move his main story forward very much, a big book of character-defining adventures still made for absorbingly pleasurable reading. I just wonder how he'll be able to bring his carefully-established threads to a suitable close in just one more book, without it feeling rushed.

0061928356|4.0|When Kavita goes to an abandoned hut to give birth, she does so hoping against hope that her new baby will be a boy.  Her first baby, a girl, was taken from her by her husband Jasu and never seen again.  In her poor farming village, a girl is a liability, no use in the fields and a drain on the husband's finances.  When the midwife hears Kavita's labor pains, she comes to assist her ... deliver another baby girl.  Kavita makes the midwife promise not to let her husband take the baby, then makes her husband promise to give her one night alone with the baby. She and her sister Rupa make the long, difficult journey to Mumbai to take the baby, whom Kavita named Usha (dawn) to an orphanage.  At least there, Kavita knows her baby will be taken care of; it's the most she can do to keep her safe.<br /><br />Meanwhile, in America, Stanford-educated doctors Somer and Krishnan have been married five years when they have a miscarriage which leads to Somer's diagnosis of early menopause at 31 years old. As she and Krishnan cope with the news, they eventually decide to adopt.  Kris's mother is a patron of the orphanage in Bombay, and they go through the long, involved process which eventually gives them their new baby, now named Asha due to an error in paperwork at the orphanage.<br /><br />This novel spans 20 years and interweaves the stories of Kavita and her family with those of Somer and hers. She never gives up on her \\"secret daughter\\", and as she eventually finds herself in the same city as the orphanage, she visits often, looking through the gate in hopes of catching a glimpse of the daughter she loved enough to give up.<br /><br />With glimpses of the lives lived in the slums of India, and of the hardships faced by women living in poverty there, contrasted with the lives of families like Krishnan's well-to-do circle, a much broader appreciation of India as a whole and the strength of it's people are readily found.<br /><br />As Asha's story comes full circle, Somer finally grows up, and as a reader, you feel pride in the woman Asha turns out to be.<br /><br />QUOTES<br /><br />Kavita is anxious, her stomach unsettled. 1601251505|4.0|A word about myself, as I believe all reviews are biased based on one's preferences and history with gaming:<br /><br />I am a loyal D&D fan and I have played the game since 1980. I play other games too, but D&D is and always will be the \\"mother ship\\" for me. I return to it frequently. I am a role player first and foremost, therefore I do not min/max and spend little time focusing on power exploits or rule mongering. I tend to GM more than actually play and I gravitate to groups that are friendly and RP-focused as well.<br /><br />This means my review is biased from the perspective of a gamer that isn't focused on power curves and is more focused on the overall elegance of the system and ease at which I can continue to use older material. I also judge on how complete the volume is, in other words, does the purchase provide everything I need as a GM/player to learn the system and start role playing right away?<br /><br />I purchased the Pathfinder CORE Rulebook very recently (May 2011) and read much of it this weekend. The purchase was made with some trepidation for the following reasons:<br /><br />1. I have a wealth of 3.5 material already, so I was unsure I even \\"needed\\" this product.<br />2. I have always been loyal to D&D proper and wanted to give 4th Edition (and WOTC) a fair shot.<br /><br />I can say, quite happily that despite these reservations I am most pleased with my purchase. Here is why:<br /><br />1. The product does improve certain aspects of 3.5. In particular combat maneuvers and grappling and the like are greatly improved and simplified and some of the classes that needed a little more punch, got tweaked in that direction. In short, Pathfinder's system is an improved revision of 3.5. So I was glad I made the investment.<br /><br />2. For me, 4th Edition is just a nightmare to adjudicate on the gaming table. For a system that bragged about its simplification, I wound up having to put all kind of pennants on our minis to track things. Also, while some of the designs and aspects of 4th Edition are interesting, the books are all fractured. I am also never quite sure what a particular 4th Edition book holds, it never seems complete. I felt, after a while, the value was just not there. So I was ready to migrate back to 3.5 and looked to Pathfinder to bring in some fresh perspective on the 3.5 system and was very pleased with what I got.<br /><br />3. In relation to points 1 & 2, Pathfinder is just simply a better value.<br /><br />4. The book is well-written, often written more clearly and concisely than the new D&D books, which often looked rushed. There are mistakes in the Pathfinder Book, but in general, its a tighter book, someone spent more time editing this book and seemed to care more about it. The D&D books, in contrast look rushed.<br /><br />5. The book has a nice style and design. Pathfinder's tone and style seems to blend traditional D&D with a 21st century update. The art is often simple, but often a perfect accent to the section or page you are reading. It features a unique blend of art, some of it appears to be acrylic/oil paintings, while other seem to be produced digitally. In contrast, the art design in 4th Edition can't compete. In particular the covers of the 4th Edition tell the story of the poor art direction, D&D looks too much like a sku you'd by at a shopping mall. Pathfinder looks like a sku you'd find at a gaming store.<br /><br />6. After purchasing the book, I was pleased to see the Pathfinder Community is rich. There is a treasure trove of web sites, forums, tool sets and utilities available online, as well as wikis, reference material and campaign guides. While 4th Edition has these as well, the more creative gamers (especially the role players, which interest me most) have all seemed to have adopted Pathfinder.<br /><br />7. My core book has Gnomes. The fact WOTC intentionally left core stuff out of their basic books, as a scheme to make you buy more crap is an insult. Insults likethis should not be rewarded with my continued business. There is some redemption in Pathfinder, it's a reward for treating gamers with respect that quality should be rewarded more readily than just a brand name.<br /><br />8. All of my 3.5 books and material fuses easily into this system, with almost little effort.<br /><br />I have complaints about the book as well. Some rules were not cleaned up or simplified as much as I would have liked. Also the Druid class seems a little broken and I am still not pleased with how Bards function. There was a movement in 3.0 to convert a Bard into a lore master, rather than someone that just sings songs to enchant/infatuate enemies. That slow conversion never progressed and in 3.75 I find the Bard's concept rather lacking. That's a very personal beef though and I would wager those who love Bards must be quite content with 3.75.<br /><br />I also think that the new rules on \\"dying\\" make Pathfinder very easy for players to survive. I don't like to kill characters regularly, but from time to time, I think a character death can accentuate the \\"danger\\" and create a little tension which only incites better RP. With the revised rules on dying and with the cleric's new 60-foot diameter healing pulse, players can survive encounters much more easily than before. As a GM, I can adjust, but I may have to house these rules a little too.<br /><br />Finally, I do not like the Reaver Miniatures that accompany Pathfinder. I understand how and why the choice was made, but I find as I age, I do not have the time to glue and paint metal figs. The metal figs I have glued and painted in the past need constant maintenance too. I desperately wish either WOTC or Paizo would explore pre-painted miniatures again. I have over 1000 of them already, but despite the fact my closet is full, I still crave more. They are not only convenient in that I do not have to glue or paint them, they even transport more effectively and rarely tarnish, break or require maintenance.<br /><br />But my misgivings are really minor compared to my overall enthusiasm for the book. The Pathfinder Core Rules is a superb value for any tabletop fantasy gamer, it presents a more elegant system than 4th Edition and it does so with an eye towards the customer: it makes sure you get everything you need and expect from your purchase.<br /><br />I don't want to say D&D has \\"jumped the shark\\", because I believe WOTC will come to its senses and eventually bring D&D back to a better place. If the retailers I know in this industry are any indication, D&D's sales are lackluster compared to where they were just 5 or 6 years ago, so the marketplace will force them to improve their quality and assess their overall direction.<br /><br />Until then, we have Paizo to guide us into the light and I am so very grateful for them for carrying the torch.'s a reward for treating gamers with respect that quality should be rewarded more readily than just a brand name.    8. All of my 3.5 books and material fuses easily into this system, with almost little effort.     I have complaints about the book as well. Some rules were not cleaned up or simplified as much as I would have liked. Also the Druid class seems a little broken and I am still not pleased with how Bards function. There was a movement in 3.0 to convert a Bard into a lore master, rather than someone that just sings songs to enchant/infatuate enemies. But my misgivings are really minor compared to my overall enthusiasm for the book. The Pathfinder Core Rules is a superb value for any tabletop fantasy gamer, it presents a more elegant system than 4th Edition and it does so with an eye towards the customer: it makes sure you get everything you need and expect from your purchase.     I don't want to say D&D has \\"jumped the shark\\", because I believe WOTC will come to its senses and eventually bring D&D back to a better place. If the retailers I know in this industry are any indication, D&D's sales are lackluster compared to where they were just 5 or 6 years ago, so the marketplace will force them to improve their quality and assess their overall direction.    Until then, we have Paizo to guide us into the light and I am so very grateful for them for carrying the torch.

B0011HOEY4|5.0|Wow.  After a glut of mediocre movies, how satisfying to find an intelligent, superbly shot and edited movie that works on several levels, and telling a true story to boot.  Denzel Washington plays Frank Lucas, the Harlem drug lord who started as driver and general right hand man to the previous drug King of Harlem.  When his boss dies, Lucas brings a business ethic - capitalist thinking that any business school would be proud of, to the business.  Rebuilding the drug trade without relying on the middle men or the Mafia, and undercutting the competition, he created a brand name - a business success story as it were.  He uses a sound business model to beat the previously untoppled opposition.  If he is presented as a superb businessman, we also see him as a violent man, almost a thug, when `necessary'.   Make no mistake - this is not a film about action scenes, but where there is violence (and that means from the very first scene) it is stark and brutal., and not for the fainthearted.  Russell Crowe on the other hand plays a cop - and in 70's Harlem, a real oddity - he's an honest cop, a man of integrity in his job.  Being an honest cop means he is the only one in a position to head up a new task force, hand picked by Crowe, to make major arrests to stem the drug flow into the city - and he sets his sights on Lucas' organisation.  What's fascinating about the movie is the comparison of the two men.  In the same way `Heat' had flawed characters that refused to be stereotyped into pure good and pure bad, we equally have realistic characters here that make you uncomfortable.  We admire Lucas's business acumen and honesty in his relationship, and find ourselves trying to excuse his bursts of violence.   And on the other hand we admire the integrity of Crowe's cop, handing in a million bucks to do `the right thing', and ultimately confronting corruption in the cops (as exemplified in Josh Brolin's caharacter) even when it means alienating himself from his colleagues... and yet at the same time his marriage is a mess as he is a serial adulterer.. so is he a man if integrity when he can't bring the same uprightness into his marriage..?  His wife feels the very pain as a victim of dishonesty of her husband at home, that he seeks so hard to stamp out at work.  It's a fascinating character study that refuses to airbrush out flaws, but those very same dichotomies in the individuals make the ending so much more understandable, and indeed, plausible.  Beyond these two, there are very few niggling problems - the wives are not well written and although important in the men's lives we never get a feel of what really makes them tick.  The length may put some people off - but I watched the extended version, which was 2 hours 38 minutes, and it flew past for me.  The highlight of the movie is probably the moment, as in Heat, when the two men meet and have their head to head.  The dnouement turns out not to be a Scarface orgy of violence, but something quite unexpected (unless you know the story already).  This movie passed me by at the cinema for some reason, and what a shame.  For this is intelligent moviemaking for adults, multilayered fare to stimulate discussion and thought.  It's not an easy watch - the violence is sudden, strong and senseless, there is nudity, sex and violence, and the characters are not cuddly white hat and dark hat ciphers.  Instead we have a morally murky movie that actually tells a layered story, a story examining both character and capitalism .  It's a `[[ASIN:B0017HRJ04 Heat [Blu-ray]]]'-like period meld of `[[ASIN:B00006JU7T Serpico (Widescreen Edition)]]' and a Harlem `[[ASIN:B0018CMJSU The Godfather - The Coppola Restoration Giftset DVD]]', depending whose viewpoint you favour in the movie, and a welcome intelligent addition to the period crime genre.

This book is not so much a biography of cancer as a history of the battles against cancer, and as such, a history of science and medicine.  We learn about the progress of medicine over the centuries, we learn about the rise of radical surgery, of devastating chemotherapy, of the politics of cancer, of the cultural history from the Jimmy Fund, to \\"if we can put a man on the moon, we can cure cancer,\\" to Lance Armstrong.  And we learn about the different worlds of searches of causes of cancer (viral (people were afraid of catching cancer), genetic, environmental), of cures (and some cures caused cancer) and for understanding the biology of cancer --- parallel universes that often failed to intersect when they should have. Mukherjee can really write, and the book does a wonderful job of following particular topics (only some mentioned above) through periods of decades, so while moving chronologically forward in general, we bounce back and forth in time as we move from subject to subject. B00466HN6I|4.0|On the surface, the trailers for Source Code give you the impression of an action film dealing with themes that have been done before in other sci-fi - and fantasy - films and shows like Groundhog Day, Quantum Leap, Frequency, The Matrix and others. And while Source Code does involve ideas that have already been done, it does it in ways that raise it head and shoulders above most films in the genre, mainly in the way its characters react to the situation they find themselves in, and in the extremely fine performances given by Jake Gyllenhaal, Michelle Monaghan, Vera Farmiga, Jeffrey Wright and a host of others. You believe in their characters, and because of that, you believe the situation they're in and you come to care about them.<br /><br />I have to be careful not to give anything away because a lot of Source Code comes in a series of revelations, so I'll limit the set-up to what you can see in the trailers. The film begins with a man named Colter Stevens (Gyllenhaal) suddenly waking up on a train, not seeming to know where he is. His confusion only increases when he finds a woman sitting across from him, Christina (Monaghan), thinks he is someone else, a friend of hers named Sean Fentress. Disoriented, Stevens makes his way to the bathroom, only to find his reflection in the mirror is that of Fentress, whose ID he finds in his wallet. His immediate confusion comes to an abrupt end moments later when the train is ripped apart by a bomb, killing him and everyone on board.<br /><br />Or so it would seem, until he wakes up again, this time in some kind of military-esque environment pod, where we learn that Stevens is an Army helicopter pilot and his last memory, prior to waking up on the train, was being shot down while on a mission in Afghanistan. On a computer screen in the pod, a female Air Force officer, Captain Goodwin (Farmiga), begins talking Stevens out of his confusion. Stevens learns that he's part of a program called Beleagured Castle, and that he's being used to try and stop a terrorist who, having blown up the train, is planning on detonating a 'dirty' nuclear device in downtown Chicago in a matter of hours.  The scientist behind Beleagured Castle, Dr. Rutledge (Wright), has devised a technique involving quantum physics which allows them to create a \\"source code\\" version of the train bombing and to insert Stevens into the body of Fentress, a passenger on the doomed train, eight minutes before the bomb goes off. And they have to keep inserting Stevens back in again and again with the mission of finding the bomb and, more importantly, finding out the identity of the bomber behind it, before the threatened detonation of the nuclear device is carried out.<br /><br />A love interest unsurprisingly develops between Stevens and Christina, but it's handled in a way that you can actually believe it. But for me, the most moving parts of the film are the interactions between Stevens and his military handler, Goodwin. Gyllenhaal and Farmiga give outstanding performances as a soldier trying desperately to understand what is happening to him and the sympathetic officer who is in the difficult position of keeping him focused on the mission while knowingly withholding information from him that could jeapordize his ability to complete it.<br /><br />A lot of talent went into Source Code and it shows.  The director, Duncan Jones (this is his second film after the highly regarded Moon) gets the pacing just right, instinctively knowing when to speed things up, when to slow them down, and how to let things unfold, always keeping you engaged with the characters.  The writer, Ben Ripley, makes his first real mark here in giving Source Code's characters a level of human depth and believability that most films of the genre lack. Ripley gives the sci-fi aspects their due, but knows that the heart of any film is in the characters and in how the audience connects with them. And the evocative musical score by Chris Bacon is deftly done, adding to the mood of a scene while never overwhelming or distracting from what's happening.<br /><br />Note: a nice nod in the film to the TV show Quantum Leap was the choice of Scott Bakula, who played Sam Beckett, as the voice of Stevens' father.<br /><br />Overall, well done, highly enjoyable, and highly, highly recommended.never overwhelming or distracting from what's happening. 0865479186|3.0|As a scientist, I have three things to say about Ben Goldacre's book *Bad Science*:    1.  He makes excellent points.  The reader, consumer, patient, doctor must pay very close attention to the details of a study before accepting any conclusions.  Fallacies are plentiful and often subtle, and he does a good job at exposing many of them.  2.  The book is poorly written.  There are better ways to present this material to a general audience.  The average reader will get the gist, but will find it difficult to follow the arguments.  3.  He does not mention a critical point:  One can poke holes in any study, because no study can possibly prove causation.  It is logically impossible to prove that two events are cause and effect.  A well-conducted study can only establish apparent correlation and even high plausibility, but not causation.  In plain language, you can never establish with certainty that a given pill will make people feel better, even on average.  But a good study will tell you whether or not a suspected cause is plausible.

Not as in \\"I just teabaged the leader in Halo\\" epic, but as in The Odyssey epic. And it's not so much fantasy as altered history. The fantasy stuff is always somewhere lurking in the back, threatening your comfort zone but keeping a respectful distance. Then you start to become curious about it's ways as it slowly creeps in. Before you know it, you want less and less lords and ladies, and more and more demons and beasts.<br /><br />Now, you may have heard the series has morally grey characters, multiple plot lines, and shocking twists by now. 0756404738|5.0|I gave this review 5 stars not because it's a perfect book, but because I enjoyed it more than I've enjoyed any other novel (aside from The Name of the Wind) in a long time. I didn't feel let down by the lack of advancement of the Chandrian plot. I certainly didn't feel like nothing happened, or that Kvothe hadn't grown. People upset that his time with Adem didn't teach him \\"discipline\\" don't seem to understand that Kvothe was largely acting the whole time. He is, first and foremost, a performer. That allows him to mold to any situation.     People's main problems with the book seem to be their expectation that this is a traditional trilogy. By Rothfuss' own admission, he pretty much shoehorned it into a trilogy format. I'm sure Rothfuss could have created smaller, better contained novels if he planned further, but that doesn't diminish my enjoyment of the story at all. It's also pretty clear that the the next book won't be the last. Even if we finish the adventures of the young Kvothe, older Kvothe still has plenty of adventures. The series also smacks of post-modernism and magical realism, and those genres simply don't conform to storytelling norms. You can see this where Kvothe the narrator skips over large portions of his story, because he decides it's unimportant.     I will agree that the Felurian part is strange, especially in comparison to the Adem part. Kvothe learns sex in exactly the same way he learns martial arts, and it's bizarre. But there are some great adventures Fae, like his acquisition of the Enshaden, or his encounter with the Ctaeth (SP?). 0061928356|5.0|Secret Daughter by Shilpi Somaya Gowda was such a great read for me! It'll be making my favorites list for this year. I'm already quite fond of novels that take me away to another place and culture and Secret Daughter does just that. This is a powerful novel that delves deeply into culture, motherhood, and a person's identity; it is both heartbreaking and uplifting.<br /><br />The novel shifts between two compelling story lines, one taking place in India and one in America. The year is 1984 and a baby girl is born to Kavita in a small village in India. Her first daughter had been taken away by her husband's family and killed. She was not going to have that happen to this one. Instead she takes her daughter to an orphanage in the hopes that she will have a better life or at the very least, a chance at one. She names her Usha which means dawn and leaves with her one silver bangle that she slips on Usha's ankle.<br /><br />A world away doctors, Somer and Krishnan, have been trying to have a child. Unfortunately it turns out that Somer is unable to have children having gone into early menopause in her thirties. She is devastated and feels betrayed by her body. Krishnan tells her that they have other options. His mother has been telling him about an orphanage in Bombay and all the children that need adopting there. As Krishnan is from India, he feels that this would be a good match for them. At first Somer is hesitant but eventually she agrees to adopt from India. They travel to India and eventually bring home their baby who is named Asha, but was born Usha.<br /><br />As the story evolves we see life from both sides. We see the agony that Kavita goes through having had to give up her daughter even though she knows it was for the best - she just wishes she knew for sure what would become of her daughter. Jasu, her husband moves them to Mumbai in the hopes of giving a better life for their son Vijay who they had the year after Usha. Life doesn't go well for them in the beginning; there are many hardships to endure, but eventually things turn around for them and life gets better. Surprisingly for how much I disliked Jasu for not wanting daughters I thought that ultimately he ended up being a good husband and really did love Kavita. No matter what was going on in their lives, whether good or bad, Kavita could not ever forget the baby girl she had to give up.<br /><br />Somer and Krishnan are having their own struggles. Well Somer is anyhow. Much of the time she really feels out of place in her own family. Asha and Krishnan have their heritage in common but she has never really been that interested in learning much about Krishnan's culture. As well she really tries to steer Asha towards life in America, clearly forgetting that the girl needs to learn about her own culture. Asha really wants to learn about her culture and meet her relatives in India - so much so that she ends up getting a grant to go to India and write a story about the underprivileged there.<br /><br />So begins Asha's search for her birth parents and her delight in meeting this family that aren't even her blood relatives but take her in and love her like one of their own. Back in America Somer and Krishnan have separated and this is just what Somer needs to get her life back. She realizes that along the way she gave up her identity and herself for her family and all she really succeeded in doing was taking away that piece of herself that made her so special. As she begins to like herself again she learns that sometimes love means compromising yourself even when it's not really what you wanted to do.<br /><br />Secret Daughter by Shilpi Somaya Gowda is a fantastic novel! I can't even pick out a favorite character because this is oneof those books in which you feel drawn to all of them. I did however feel more drawn to the story line in India but only because I loved to read about a culture that is so different from my own. There are some parts of the book that I found upsetting like what happens to baby girls and just how women are treated in general but that is part of India and the story. B002VECLVO|5.0|There are movies in which Colin Firth's ability to stony and almost emotionless countenance really fits the part. A\\" A Single Man\\" is a glorious example. Firth plays George Falconer, an British teacher of college English in 1962 Los Angeles. We know the year because televisions and radios endless broadcast stories about the Cuban Missile Crisis. We learn almost immediately that Falconer is gay, and that his lover and friend, Jim, has been killed in a traffic accident. Bereft, Falconer becomes suicidal. These include friendships with Charley (Julianne Moore), a boozy, spurned woman whose husband dumped her for someone younger and more beautiful. Falconer also meets a number of gay and maybe-gay men, including one of his students, who become touch points and opportunities to reminisce about his now-lost relationship. Some might think this an obvious or cloying visual device, but Falconer's inner moods are shown by the saturation of color on the screen. When he is engaged with another person, the colors are vibrant; when he becomes morose, the colors are muted. A repeating motif of Firth floating nude in a dark sea also becomes a clue to his inner listlessness and despair.<br /><br />\\"A Single Man\\" explores the grief of a man who is not allowed to grieve publicly for the man he loved. The early 1960s were still an extremely buttoned-up era for homosexuals. They rendered their sexuality invisible as a means of self-protection, and were years away from beginning to assert their rights to be seen as persons. Firth's choice as Falconer was nearly perfect; sometimes it seems he can't depict ANY emotions. The plotline took the characters to unexpected and mostly unfulfilled places. A moving and often sad story of a grieving man's attempting to break the surface of his own despair and loss, and of a society unwilling to let him.

B001DCQI8W|5.0|This is unquestionably one of Karajan's greatest achievements. And it's hard to imagine a better Bruckner cycle than this. I've listened to Bruckner for a long time and own at least 5 recordings of each symphony. Every one of these performances ranks up there with the best (I must single out the performance of the 5th, absolutely THE best I've ever heard!). There is not a weak performance in the box. And you simply will not hear these symphonies played better than here. The Berlin Philharmonic is glorious. Karajan was often accused of producing beauty just for beauty's sake, but here it is always at the service of the music. In these grand, epic works where other conductors can get lost or lose tension in certain areas, Karajan has an incredible ability to comprehend the structure of each movement, keep it flowing, and make each transition from section to section sound effortless and natural. A great term I once heard to describe it was \\"architectural far-sightedness\\". Tempo choices throughout strike me as having an ideal balance of energy and grandeur. And the all important Adagios have plenty of time to breathe without ever dragging.     There have been some slightly negative comments about the sound quality. I thought the analog recordings (4-9) were excellent. All the sections of the orchestra were brilliantly caught. I would say some of the big climaxes in the symphonies recorded in early digital (1-3) sound a little harsh and congested, but nothing major enough to detract from the performances. Also, Karajan uses the 1889 version of the 3rd. Though I prefer the 1873 or 1877 versions, it's no big deal.     Whether you know Bruckner well, or you've never heard a note he's written, I give this set my strongest possible recommendation. It's a perfect introduction to this music, and a perfect addition to any Classical library. (And getting this 9 disc set for 40 bucks is just robbery!)
1400052181|4.0|Some stories stick with you, marinating, festering, always present. They might piss you off, make you happy, confuse you, but you keep thinking about them. Henrietta Lacks' story is like that.<br /><br />As a young mother suffering from cancer in a segregated US, Lacks unknowingly changed the course of medical history. But her identity and contribution to science remained a secret to most of the world.<br /><br />In 1976, the American magazine Rolling Stone featured an article about Henrietta Lacks and her family. They were also the subject of a 1996 documentary by the BBC called The Way of All Flesh and articles in Ebony and Science80. But not until the publishing of The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks, by Rebecca Skloot, has the whole story been shared.<br /><br />To explain the tremendous impact Lacks had, it is essential to go into the details of her medical history and of medical research. Though that sets up the possibility for a very dull narrative, Skloot deftly explains the material and focuses on the human element in such a way that readers will be sucked in.<br /><br />In doing research for this story, Skloot painstakingly gains the trust of Lacks' surviving children, especially her daughter Deborah. Through interviews with them, relatives, physicians and researchers, a story slowly builds.<br />In the 1950s Lacks was treated for a cancer that was later diagnosed as cervical cancer. Although treated in a segregated ward at Johns Hopkins in Baltimore, Maryland, she received care that was considered to be the best available for African Americans. Despite this, Lacks died as a result of her cancer at the age of 31, leaving behind five children and her husband.<br /><br />At that same at John Hopkins, George Gey was busily researching how to keep cells alive and growing. It wasn't until Gey received a sample of Lacks' cells that he found success. And a lot of it. Lacks' cells, named HeLa after the first two initials in Lacks' first and last names, grew at a great rate and didn't stop. These cells were a huge scientific discovery that would change medical research and patient rights forever.<br /><br />After a few false starts, Gey developed a way to send samples of HeLa around the world. Researchers were able to use them to create treatments for cancer and AIDS. They made it possible to do tests on the polio vaccine, effects of radiation and safety of various everyday products. Researchers also challenged the rights of patients, experimenting by injecting live patients will HeLa (cancer), which led to more stringent regulations being created.<br /><br />The part of Lacks' story that stuck in the mind of her sons was this: Although Johns Hopkins didn't make any money off of HeLa, other people around the world did. Lots of money. And the Lacks family not only didn't get a percentage of it, they didn't even know that the cells had been taken. The family was poor with no health insurance and several members were battling illnesses. Medicine was profiting from their relative's cells but they weren't getting to enjoy the benefits of medicine.<br /><br />The part that Lacks' daughter Deborah couldn't help but focus on: Via her cells, Lacks was having an enormous global effect and adding to scientific knowledge, but her own daughter didn't have a single memory of Lacks.<br />Although Skloot gives voice to the sons, the focus of The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks, due to the influence of Deborah, is Henrietta.<br /><br />The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks, Skloot's first book, takes readers on a journey through history. Readers will be happy to have found this book and to learn of HeLa, which plays such an important role in medicine and research and continue to live on and thrive and to learn about the woman behind those cells.a huge scientific discovery that would change medical research and patient rights forever.    After a few false starts, Gey developed a way to send samples of HeLa around the world. Researchers were able to use them to create treatments for cancer and AIDS. They made it possible to do tests on the polio vaccine, effects of radiation and safety of various everyday products. Researchers also challenged the rights of patients, experimenting by injecting live patients will HeLa (cancer), which led to more stringent regulations being created.    The part of Lacks' story that stuck in the mind of her sons was this: Although Johns Hopkins didn't make any money off of HeLa, other people around the world did. Lots of money. And the Lacks family not only didn't get a percentage of it, they didn't even know that the cells had been taken. The family was poor with no health insurance and several members were battling illnesses. Medicine was profiting from their relative's cells but they weren't getting to enjoy the benefits of medicine.    The part that Lacks' daughter Deborah couldn't help but focus on: Via her cells, Lacks was having an enormous global effect and adding to scientific knowledge, but her own daughter didn't have a single memory of Lacks.  Although Skloot gives voice to the sons, the focus of The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks, due to the influence of Deborah, is Henrietta.    The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks, Skloot's first book, takes readers on a journey through history. Readers will be happy to have found this book and to learn of HeLa, which plays such an important role in medicine and research and continue to live on and thrive and to learn about the woman behind those cells.

The book is a nice size which is great. It is a hardcover, and the pages are thick and very nice quality. The colors and art can be appreciated with the bigger size and the better quality paper.     The content is beyond incredible. The art in the book comes with blurbs about the piece from the artist or a producer, and it often goes into detail about the piece, why it was made, and what the goal for the piece was. Most of the work is concept art, but they are of high quality. There is alot of complete pieces with full color and depth. Not just sketches or storyboards which is common in alot of artbooks you get as preorder bonuses with games.  The book is broken into chapters, with each chapter giving insight on an aspect of the game, such as preproduction or Wonderland locations. Each chapter has art pertaining to the theme. So in preproduction, you see alot of the brainstorming and ideas that came up early in the game's development that ended up getting cut from the game. It's a real treat to see. Even the design of the characters are in detail.    The book is about 170 pages or so, and has a ton of material to go through. I could not put the book down, and finished it in a day. It was just really good and kept my interest up the whole time.    If I have any small gripes, it is that a few of the pieces which are VERY interesting, have no blurbs about them. There were a couple of pictures I had really hope had some context to it, but it only lists who did them. About 5% of the pictures have no blurbs. Also, some of the artwork isn't talked about enough. For example, there is a really neat picture released when the game was announced of alice scaling a tower crawling with snails and a mechanical snail at the top. The piece is here, but is only briefly discussed. Another minor gripe is that book isa bit spoilerish in that it shows some of the new characters and the new look of original characters. I couldn't fathom an artbook without ALL the material either, so I am glad it wasn't cut out for the sake of being spoilerish. I do wish there was a disclaimer somewhere though.     Still, this is an excellent book, and one I enjoyed. I hope you enjoy it too!

B00003CXBK|5.0|As always, Miyazaki delivers another masterpiece with Princess Mononoke. Set in 14th century Japan, it tells the story of a young prince named Ashitaka who must leave his people to find a cure for a curse he receives while defending his village from a boar-demon. He finds Iron Town and meets Lady Eboshi, a warrior women who runs the town and mines for iron. Her goal: to kill the forest god so no one will be able to stop her men from mining. Meanwhile, San (Princess Mononoke - who was raised by Moro the wolf god - is desperately trying to defend the forest from the humans who would try and destroy it.    There is no black and white villain in the story. The most likely candidate, Lady Eboshi, does try to kill the forest spirits, but also buys women out of brothels and gives them honest work. She also protects the lepers, those that would otherwise be complete outcasts with no way to support them. Although the story is set in the time of Japanese history that we normally romanticize (samurais, princesses in kimonos and emperors trying to avert assassination attempts, etc.) Miyazaki sets his story in a rural setting, where the emperor never appears in person, and samurais attack innocent villages. And of course, Miyazaki presents what is perhaps his most outright campaign for environmentalism. The forest he creates is large and wild, filled with gods and spirits that hate humans. However, to those that do not seek to harm the forest, the spirits can be fierce allies.     Overall, I would rate this as a very excellent movie. However, some parents might be concerned that there is too much violence and gore for their children. Yes, this is Miyazaki's most \\"graphic\\" movie, but it is limited to very specific scenes and emphasizes the point he is trying to make. War and progress often only result in the destruction of that which is most beautiful: nature and the human soul.

0375842209|5.0|Oh.  My.  God.  Words fail when it comes to describing this book.  I can't say I wasn't expecting that part of it - I'm a sucker for WWII cry-your-eyes-out kind of books - but the way this book took my breath away was enough to take my breath away a second time.  More than anything else, it's a story about childhood, not a story about battles or mass murder or tragedy, and that's what makes it different from anything else out there.    \\"Put this on the shelf next to The Diary of Anne Frank,\\" I've heard some reviews say.  That's sort of true, as far as one, how good it is, and two, its ability to make you cry until you could be wrung out, but I think they're missing the point in lumping the two together.  I think Anne Frank might have been a little disappointed at how few people seem willing to make the jump beyond her own tragedy into the tragedy of the entire war.  The subtitle, after all, is Diary of a Young Girl, and that's what The Book Thief reads like - a diary.  Only it's Death's diary, about a young girl.  And when you're a young girl, you don't much care if you're German or Dutch or Jewish or Catholic.    That was another thing I liked about the story, beyond the brilliance of the writing or anything else - how it humanized ordinary Germans.  I am disgusted by what the Nazis did as much as the next person is, but not all Germans were Nazis - and many, many Germans did valiant, dangerous things to defend their principles.  The Book Thief throws those things into sharp, heartrending relief.    And I hope I haven't neglected to mention that the writing was beyond, beyond, BEYOND belief.  I mean, Death as a narrator?  Had most authors tried it, it would have come across as unbelievably hokey.  In Markus Zusak's hands, it's a beautiful thing, and I can't imagine the story being told any other way.  There's been a lot of talk of unreliable narrators in the writing world, lately, and in my opinion, this is the real deal.    One of the best books I have read all year; probably one of the best books I have read in my entire life.  (I've probably read thousands of books, so don't take that lightly.)  Just read it!
B0041KKYEM|3.0|Darren Aronofsky made such a bold, provocative debut with \\"Pi\\" back in 1998. But his career has been nothing but overwrought un-realism ever since. \\"Black Swan\\" is an uninspired rehash of Roman Polanski's superb \\"Repulsion\\" (1965), about an apparently schizophrenic, sexually repressed young woman who descends into madness amid paranoid hallucinations and libidinous men. \\"Repulsion\\" is a great film, while \\"Black Swan\\" is overwrought, gimmicky, not the least bit credible, and badly acted -not that it matters considering the superficiality of the exercise. It's also a ballet movie with hardly any ballet in it. Natalie Portman is in great shape but looks absurdly strained performing the simplest of movements.<br /><br />Nina Sayers (Natalie Portman) is an emotionally disturbed young ballerina who seems to be living out the ambitions of her domineering mother (Barbara Hershey). Nevertheless, she is the hardest-working ballerina in her company, technically perfect, but lacking a certain passion. She dreams of dancing the Swan Queen in \\"Swan Lake\\", and, to her surprise and everyone else's, the company's choreographer Thomas Leroy (Vincent Cassel) gives her the part. Mr. Leroy is confident in Nina's ability to play the White Swan but is concerned that she lacks the sexual energy and transcendence to play the Black Swan. The problem preoccupies Nina as well, and the stress erodes her grasp on reality and brings on increasingly violent psychotic episodes.<br /><br />Natalie Portman does not have the acting chops for this role. She does not know how to convey emotion except by emoting. She does the entire film with furrowed brow. A better actress could have made Nina's torment believable but not her situation. It's simply not credible that this weak, flagrantly unstable person would possess the single-minded drive of a professional ballet dancer, much less that anyone would give her the lead in a production. She looks like she's going to have an anxiety attack in every scene. Mila Kunis, on the other hand, is charismatic as rival ballerina Lily, who seems to be genuinely trying to be friendly and helpful to Nina despite the latter's paranoia. Kunis' sassy, lewd and tattooed temptress screams out for more screen time than she gets.<br /><br />Instead, we get crotch shots, hallucinations, CGI that allows Nina to sprout feathers, and very little ballet. On the bright side, \\"Black Swan\\" gets better as it goes along, because the story grows into Natalie Portman's panicked performance. By the final act, Nina has reason to break down under the stresses of her paranoia, hallucinations, and determination to become the Black Swan. The film is saved by good pacing, a suspenseful final act, and a climactic ending. But one lesson that filmmakers should find in \\"Black Swan\\" is that there is little point in setting a movie in a ballet company if there isn't going to be ballet dancing in it. Another is that a character's personality has to suit her role. And don't expect untalented actresses to be able to carry a dramatic film for an hour and 50 minutes.the other hand, is charismatic as rival ballerina Lily, who seems to be genuinely trying to be friendly and helpful to Nina despite the latter's paranoia. B003E6K1VK|4.0|First and foremost, I can say that for my purposes this device works perfectly, and I have no complaints whatsoever about quality -- what is not a common occurrence. However this is at large extent because I needed exactly this kind of device -- a USB DAC with a headphones amplifier that I can connect to my computer to drive [[ASIN:B000UVGI6O Audio-Technica ATH-M50 Studio Monitor Headphones]] (and deal with their low impedance), or amplified speakers, without having to deal with noise, distortion or weirdness of onboard audio adapter. The device works as advertised -- reproduces sound in a way that I can't find any flaws with either my ears or the minimal amount of measurements that I have performed. If the user wishes, it adds slightly more bass (to compensate for headphones' frequency response -- though ATH-M50 don't seem to need any). It has manual volume control with 60 steps, 50 being unity gain (output equal to the input).    Those very things may be exactly the opposite of someone else's expectations -- for high-impedance headphones with audio source that only provides analog output (or audio source that can drive low-impedance headphones on its own), this device is a $100 equivalent of a straight piece of wire. I don't have an iPod/iPhone, so I can't evaluate what this amplifier adds or does not add when connected to it, or what happens with lower impedance headphones (ATH-M50 is 38 Ohms), but I would not be surprised if the answer is \\"nothing\\". If you have something with analog output that does not strain itself trying to drive your headphones, you probably won't like this device (as an amplifier) at all.    On the other hand, if you have a laptop with headphones jack that mostly produces noise and distortion, you will benefit from this more than I did -- this device has a high-quality DAC, you will have the benefit of having all the noise from other components remaining far away from analog audio path, and the amplifier is most likely much better than whatever is built into the laptop. That is -- if you connect it to a USB port so it will replace the existing audio adapter completely. Connecting it to a USB-capable computer in the \\"amplifier only\\" mode would be a waste of time because it will amplify already distorted signal. It will still help if computer couldn't drive the headphones at any usable level or causes more distortion with low-impedance headphones compared to high-impedance amplifier or allows you to use a separate un-amplified line output to connect the amplifier, but if you have USB port, it makes no sense, DAC in this device is certainly better at producing analog signal than anything in your computer, and even if by any chance it's the same, it still operates in a less noisy environment.    I have never used it with a CD player, but I guess, it will work well as an amplifier -- if you need one to drive your headphones. It will not turn 16-bit 44100Hz of a CD into something else, just like it won't turn 64Kbps MP3 into CD-quality sound, and it will not make your headphones nearly burst your eardrums all the while preserving the sound perfectly. It certainly won't fix flaws of the recording itself, especially those that you can notice with nice headphones (I count ATH-M50 as \\"nice\\"). I realize that some \\"audiophiles\\" can literally pay $100 for a straight piece of wire that does nothing, and/or spend hours discussing what that straight piece of wire supposedly does and how it compares to other straight pieces of wire, but at very least I am compelled to say that this amplifier will be exactly the same thing if used where it is not needed.    Another limitation that may be important for some, is lack of the recording input. Usually USB audio adapters come with both DAC for output and ADC for input. Quality of ADC often being more important than DAC because it's used to record audio for various kinds of processing, and processing loses precision. This one is output-only -- if you use computer for recording, you will need something else for this purpose (USB microphone, dedicated ADC, line input on existing audio adapter, assuming that quality is sufficient...) Not being a musician or singer, I don't care, my USB microphone is sufficient for those rare situations when the sound of my voice is supposed to appear somewhere in a digital form.    Now, to my own complaints. There are two:    1. High-gloss plastic front panel. Should be very easy to scratch. Ships with soft (and usually already scratched) transparent plastic sheet on it, that the user is supposed to replace with a much more pleasant-looking and durable sheet that comes with device. I have failed miserably to perform this procedure despite having no problems doing exactly the same on other devices. No idea why -- maybe there was just too much dust in the air -- but the only available sheet was gone. I ended up buying third-party protective sheet. Why front panel is like this in the first place, is a mystery for me -- it's not a touchscreen, the rest of the device is in a very durable case, there is a rubber-like cover that covers everything except for this shiny front surface -- despite the fact that only a small fraction of it is used to display anything.    2. No useful documentation. What was not a big deal for all situations except one -- when I have discovered that volume control from computer does not work. DAC is correctly recognized by ALSA drivers (I run Linux), volume control shows up just like on all other audio adapters, however it does absolutely nothing -- volume stays at whatever level is set on the device with +/- buttons. To solve this mystery, I looked at the specifications and found that USB DAC part of the device uses two chips -- TI PCM2706 and Wolfson WM8740. Not having used those two before, but knowing that TI chip is widely supported without any problems, I went to TI web site and got its datasheet. That immediately explained what was going on. PCM2706 is a full USB DAC -- it provides USB interface, analog output and everything in between. Except, FIIO only uses it as a USB interface, and actual DAC is Wolfson -- it's more precise (less noise and harmonic distortion), what is one of the reasons why this device is expensive.    TI chip has analog output and two digital output interfaces, however only digital output from TI chip is connected anywhere, and trying to control volume of unused analog output is pointless. Once I knew what to look for, I have found that indeed E7 is recognized as a device with two outputs -- digital and analog. Switching to the digital device solved the problem, ALSA recognized that it has to apply gain by itself (in software) to change the volume.    I realize that manufacturer expects most users to be completely unaware of those options, especially when amplifier itself has its own volume buttons and display. I don't even know how it all looks under Windows or OSX -- maybe they do everything in software even if better analog controls are available. But it would be nice if there was something mentioned about it in the manual -- just for those unfortunate who want to have all controls work perfectly but can't read circuit diagrams or don't know what kind of interface has a signal named \\"LRCK\\". Something like:    \\"Though all outputs of this device are analog, software configuration, if any, should treat this device as digital stereo output (usually marked as \\"S/PDIF\\" or \\"IEC958\\" ), not analog audio output, because digital audio is used internally between USB adapter and DAC\\".    It's not even important that internal interface is I2S, not S/PDIF itself, the important part is that anything controlling S/PDIF will do the right thing but controlling analog output (like in other USB-connected DACs) will not.

0452295548|3.0|I wanted to like this book, but I found the writing very uneven - it's part memoir, part New Age self-help manual with a little bit of junior high school science thrown in, and it doesn't really hit the mark on any of them.    The detailed description of Dr. Taylor's experience of the stroke - her feelings and disjointed thought processes throughout - was the most compelling section for me. Unfortunately, that kind of attention to detail is lacking in her account of the extended recovery process, which basically amounts to \\"I couldn't do X, so I kept trying. And then I slept a lot. And then I tried some more.\\" We don't get much of a sense of the challenges or frustrations involved in her therapy; rather, the focus is on length of time - how long she had to sleep to recover her energy, and how long it took her to recover words, visual integration, motor coordination and ultimately, the rest of her faculties.    The neuroanatomy of brain function and stroke is presented in a very simplistic way in the earlier chapters, but there is a complete lack of scientific content in the latter half of the book, which expounds on the author's feelings of Nirvana and being \\"one with the universe\\" due to the dominance of her not-so-damaged right hemisphere. We're given a blurry reproduction or two of Dr. Taylor's diagnostic brain scans right after the stroke, but we don't get to see the progression of her recovery charted in subsequent scans, nor do we get even a brief contrast study of a patient with right-hemispheric damage and how that changes stroke symptoms, treatment and prognosis.    This might be excusable were it not for the author's tendency to espouse unproven \\"universal truths\\" such as sending out \\"good vibrations\\" based primarily on her subjective experiences. Dr. Taylor's advice to \\"Step to the right\\" (in neurological terms, that is) to achieve true happiness would be a lot more compelling if I could see some rigorous science behind it instead of a subjective personalaccount of magically finding bliss after a devastating neurological event.    For those interested in neuroplasticity, I suggest [[ASIN:0143113100 The Brain That Changes Itself: Stories of Personal Triumph from the Frontiers of Brain Science (James H. Silberman Books)]] by Norman Doidge, who does a much better job of integrating science with medical anecdote.
0743487486|5.0|As a Marriage and Family Therapy graduate student, I read Unconditional Parenting on a recommendation from a professor.  While admittedly, I am not yet a parent (and understand how some will disregard my opinion due to this), part of me is happy that I read this book before having children.  I am happy because after reading Kohn's piece, I feel more equipped to be a parent who is respectful, supportive, assumes the best in children, and nurtures learning.    In Unconditional Parenting, Kohn thoroughly describes the problematic-nature of popular, control-based parenting styles that use rewards and punishment to obtain child obedience.  Using research, Kohn demonstrates that \\"doing to\\" parenting is not only counter-productive in some instances, yet also can hinder moral development, teach children to use power to get what they want, and create obsessive compliance.  Instead of this conditional approach, Kohn proposes a \\"being with\\" style that includes:   * Reconsidering your requests- problem may be in what you are asking your child to do  * Putting the relationship first- creates comfort to talk and trust  * Loving unconditionally- show children they do not have to earn parent's love  * Asking how things seem from child's point of view?  * Being authentic- apologize, do not act infallible  * Talking less, asking more  * Attributing to children the best possible motive consistent with the facts  * Trying to say \\"yes\\" when possible  * Being flexible  * Letting kids decide unless there is a compelling otherwise- give them the first word because they do not have the last word    Easy to do? Absolutely not. Beneficial? It seems so.

So my final question is about writing for the pleasure of the prose we produce. I sense that you are addicted to the unfolding puzzle nature of a story: could you write a book with me as your IR? [that's Ideal Reader for those who have not yet read On Writing]. You could be my IR when I finally sit down with the blinds down, the door closed and write my novel. B0000029DD|4.0|I HATED JEFF Buckley. Well, at least I did when his first single, \\"Last Goodbye,\\" came out while I was rocking my face off to sounds from Pearl Jam, the Smashing Pumpkins, Sound Garden, and Stone Temple Pilots. However, as the mind matures, so does the openness to other forms of art and music, and after fifteen years I see him as one of the most influential singer/songwriters in contemporary popular music. Jeff Buckley is an eclectic, guitar playing singer from the 1990's, whose only major studio album, Grace (1994), is a highly acclaimed success by critics and musicians from a wide variety of genres.    The original version of Grace has ten tracks on it that range from hard rock songs like \\"Eternal Life\\" to melodic acoustic anthems such as \\"Love, Should've Come Over.\\" Buckley also covers three songs on the album: \\"Lilac Wine\\"; a beautiful, mellow, jazzy tune that moves from a dark, dissident melody to a falsetto-like triumph; \\"Corpus Christi Carol\\" that exemplifies Buckley's vocal ability and range, but is completely alien from the other tracks on the album; and, the gem of all is \\"Hallelujah,\\"  which was originally written by poet/songwriter Leonard Cohen, but which Buckley makes the ballad his own by taking out the last two verses of Cohen's original and sings three alternate verses that make the song provocative and sexy, yet sad. An example of Buckley's sensuality is in the forth verse:     well there was a time when you let me know   what's really going on below   but now you never show that to me do you   but remember when I moved in you   and the holy dove was moving too   and every breath we drew was hallelujah      Besides the covers, Buckley's original sound is widely varied but extremely ahead of the times - even by today's standards. His melodies, vocal sound, guitar work, and arrangements are unique, but not too eclectic to cause distaste to the listener. I would highly recommend anybody to buy this album. It may take some time for you to fallin love with it. It did for me. But I am certain that some measure of Jeff Buckley's Grace will touch one's heart.       It's a shame that Buckley can no longer make music due to his untimely death by drowning in the Mississippi River in 1997; however, the man has left us with something legendary--a masterpiece that may be found to be \\"all over the place\\" when listened to the first few times, but like that of age, your mind, heart, and soul will be moved by at least one aspect of this exceptional artist--Jeff Buckley.

Personally, I don't care that much about plot. As long as interesting characters are doing interesting things in desperate situations, I'm happy. Just keep it interesting. In book one, Kvothe brought me to tears when he learned to play the lute and struggled to survive in Tarbean. And the events at university were so fast-paced and brilliant I couldn't put the book down. Although there are plenty of good moments in Wise Man's Fear, nothing comes close to the beauty and exuberance of the first book.<br /><br />And as Kvothe transforms from mischievous prodigy to loutish lothario, I found myself losing interest in him. His final, offhand, out-of-the-blue act of malice toward Ambrose was the last straw for me. I'll be cheering for Ambrose in book three.<br /><br />N.B.: I've been reading through the reviews and notice that many people love Devi and dislike Denna. An interesting question is how Devi would respond to Kvothe if he spoke to her the way he speaks to Denna. My guess is she'd vomit on his shoes and set his blood sample alight. The problem is that Kvothe and Denna are star-crossed lovers and therefore not allowed to speak sensibly to one another. It's all flowers in midnight gardens for these two, with the occasional spat thrown in to drag their story out for several thousand pages. Shakespeare could be florid too, but he had the good sense to give us just one balcony scene.
0143116177|3.0|This is a lively but superficial history of finance. Niall Ferguson surveys the evolution of credit and debt from ancient Mesopotamia to the Medicis in Renaissance Florence to the corporate finance bases of the Dutch, British and American empires. His chapters trace the rise of money and credit, the bond market, the stock market, insurance, the real estate market, and the rises and falls of international finance.<br /><br />Ferguson never asks where the money comes from, never asks who produces the world's wealth. Maybe he knows the truth - the world's workers produce the wealth and the world's capitalists nick it.<br /><br />Argentina's default in 2001 was the biggest in history. 500,000 creditors had to accept new bonds worth 35 cents on the dollar. As Ferguson writes, \\"So successful did its default prove (economic growth has since surged ...) that many economists were left to ponder why any sovereign debtor ever honours its commitments to foreign bondholders.\\" Default really is good for you - unless you are a bondholder.<br /><br />Ferguson points out, \\"It's not owning property that gives you security; it just gives your creditors security. Real security comes from having a steady income.\\" Capitalism can't achieve this - witness the 20 per cent youth unemployment here and the 45 per cent youth unemployment in Spain.<br /><br />Countries, like individuals, need to have a diversified portfolio of assets, and must not put all their eggs into one basket, as Ireland did with its insane property bubble and bust.<br /><br />Ferguson is so love in capitalism that he skates over its huge bubbles and busts, its vast injustices, its dependence on exploiting the great majority of the world's people, and the huge extent of world poverty.

1589949811|4.0|This is the core set of Fantasy Flight Game's new Living Card Game set in J.R.R. Tolkien's fascinating universe.    Background:  Set in the time after the events of the Hobbit and before The Lord of the Rings this new card game aims to deliver a new set of stories that evolve around the characters of Tolkien's Middle Earth. As this is a Living Card Game, the core set provides just an introductory set of cards, that will be increased with monthly expansion packs.    Overview of the Game:  The Game can be played with 1 - 4 players, the interesting aspect here being the game can be played solo. Also this is a cooperative game, so the players join forces together in order to defeat the evil that lurks within Mirkwood.   The Core set provides a set of 12 heroes and four according decks to start right away. Each of the decks covers one sphere and corresponds to a triplet of heroes. Spheres are thematic compounds to which all cards except Gandalf belong.   The four Spheres are: Leadership, Tactics, Spirit and Lore. They each have quite a different feel and style of play and provide a lot of flavor and diversity.   Once the players set up their decks, the aim is to meet certain requirements provided on quest cards. Once the requirements of a quest card are met, the heroes move on to the next stage in a quest (represented by the next quest cards). The quests in the core set have three stages, once the third stage is completed, the heroes win the game. Should the heroes be defeated or succumb to the evil powers (represented by a threat count for each player) they will lose the game.   Of course the heroes will face hordes of enemies and evil sorcery and have to explore a variety of locations in order to complete these quests. Luckily, there are dozens of equipments, spells and allies to assist the heroes in the direst hours.     Critique:  The game has quite a new feel to it, it seems the developers put a lot of thought into creating a new game experience and succeeded well-enough in doing so. The mechanics are interesting and the tension rises as players move along the path (represented by quest cards). The four spheres provide a very diverse game play and the free possibility to create decks using the cards of the core set allows for even more variety, as spheres can be combined and different hero-combinations can be explored.   However, the core set provides only little cards to allow for much options in deck building. According to the rules a tournament deck should consist of 50 cards, but the core set doesn't provide enough cards for each sphere to create a valid 50 card deck for each sphere. (The starter decks only consist of 30 cards each). Thus, the deck building possibilities are very limited, which is a bummer given the extreme difficulty level of the quests. Also the game experience changes a lot depending on what cards are drawn of the encounter deck, adding a lot of randomness to the game. Thus, sometimes a victory doesn't feel like a real victory, but more like a lucky streak. With the current selection of cards, defeating the second and third quests reliably in solo-player is impossible and thus the much advertised single player mode of the game fails to deliver. A pity, since mechanic-wise everything would be there for a great adventure alone or together with friends. Buying more core sets to increase the number of cards would solve the shortcomings of deck building possibilities.    The adventure packs:  The adventure will provide a monthly addition of new cards. So far the first one has yet to be released, but according to ffg the packs will consist of one new scenario (quest), one new hero card, nine unique player cards (in sets of three) and an encounter deck for the new quest. The adventure packs will be grouped in cycles of six packs that form together with the core set a thematic unit. The current cycle is the Shadows of Mirkwood cycle. Considering the addition of only nine new cards (distributed among four spheres) with each adventure pack, the lack of cards of the core set will not be undone quickly and players have to be quite patient before some real deck building can be done.     Summary:  The game feels great. The mechanics and the flavor of the setting provide both: A fun gaming experience and a great story. Due to the limited number of cards the deck building aspect of the game is somewhat a joke and given the extreme level of difficulty I highly recommend to find some friends and tackle to quests together. The game comes to full fruition when played with two or three players. (Basically one core set provides enough material for four players, there might be too few tokens and there are only two threat counters in each core set).   The adventure packs will further the story and provide new quests and cards and hopefully thicken the somewhat thin experience the core set provides.

1607886278|4.0|Room is one of those books that is hard to review. The subject matter is so aggravating and repulsive that a glowing evaluation almost seems like an endorsement of kidnapping, imprisonment and repeated sexual assault. Despite the undertones, Room is seldom graphic and I found it to be captivating and entertaining. Yes, the shell is rough, but in the pulpy middle once Jack and Ma work their way into your heart, a passionate portrayal of love and survival is revealed. That the story is told from Jack's 5-year old perspective is probably it's most riveting characteristic, and it's most aggravating. His voice is innocent and understated. The things that happen to him in room and even outside of it, he takes as a matter of course, having little else to compare life to. For a worldly reader, you may want to reach in and shake the boy from time to time and tell him that isn't how life has to be. I resisted a compulsion to second-guess Ma and her every decision, deciding instead to play through the entire novel first. At the end, I don't know if her decisions were the right ones, but I know that they were fair from her perspective. Not many of us could have ever said we'd walked in her shoes. Now on May 13, 2011 Clark Kent's journey is coming to an end. It's bittersweet for me, as much as I would love for it to continue it's time for Clark to become Superman. I honestly feel the show over the past couple of seasons has been the best it's ever been and it's good to go out with a bang! The good thing about Smallville is that you know it never really ends; in fact the ending is the beginning which is inspiring. Though perhaps I have been a bit of a wish-washy fan over the years I have been a fan none of the less, so this Friday I will not be at my usual bar, I will be on my couch at 8:00 as I watch Clark Kent for the last (and hopefully the first) time take flight. B000OPPBEQ|5.0|Like many who have purchased this collection, I was totally blown away by the original film when I saw it opening weekend in 1999, truly an experience I had not had since seeing the original Star Wars when it opened. And, like so many, I waited with incredible anticipation for the sequels...and came out both times not exactly knowing what to think. Over time I grew to have great disdain for Reloaded and Revolutions, trying desparately to watch the original and cling to the fantasy of the sequels never existing.  Like Cypher, I wanted to be re-instered into the Matrix with no memory of what happened after I took the second red pill in 2003. I therefore passed on this collection on DVD, and gave serious thought to only getting the original on Blu. Then the price came down and I thought the original movie, the Animatrix and all those extras were worth the cost even if I never watched the sequels again. However, some time on my hands lately helped me decide to go ahead and delve back in, after several years of having not watched Reloaded or Revolutions. I must say that having had the time to temper my expectations and perceptions, I actually very much enjoyed the trilogy as a whole. My present day thoughts on each movie:    The Matrix - How can I heap more superlatives than countless hours of analysis have already given us? Suffice to say this movie is and was awesome. It broke rules and created a standard sci-fi action movies of today still struggle to meet. It definitely made the flaws of one of those other major releases (cough*worst Star Wars film ever*cough) of that year stand out even more. This is a classic in excellent form with great picture quality and extras...5 stars all the way!    Reloaded - For me, time has really been kind to this oft-maligned sequel. It took many of my preconceptions and ideas about where the story would or should go and basically flushed them like a freed pod-born. That used to be one of my criticisms, but I have to say now I actuallyappreciate these unexpected twists. It certainly still has its flaws, though: 1) Too many underdeveloped characters. I honestly didn't care much about what happened to most of these people, and that sucked given how much screen time they steal in Revolutions (but I am moving ahead prematurely). 2) Too much action...yes, there is such a thing. For all of the awesome effects-filled set pieces in both sequels, they at times become overlong and over-indulgent. It only needs to be that long if I have a controller in my hand! 3)The rave scene...does anyone besides a twelve year old boy (or someone with that mindset) like this scene? I realize it's supposed to be a life-affirming act and all, but it just comes off as rather poorly done soft-core porn.  Aside from these gripes, I found myself really into the narrative shift of Reloaded, the discussions about choice and where the path of the one would ultimately lead. This movie would probably have gotten about a 2 or 3 star rating from me back when, but today it gets a solid 4.5 stars.    Revolutions - No matter how much time I give it, this one remains my biggest disappointment of the trilogy (but hey I still say that about Return of the Jedi as well). Still, I did enjoy it much more with my recent viewing. So many subtle things I missed (or refused to see) in its narrative. My biggest issue? For all its visual splendor, the battle for Zion still kind of bores me...as I mentioned, I really didn't connect with the characters fighting the \\"war\\" and so just kind of marked time until we returned to what was going on with Neo and Trinity (or Morpheus and company). Given that was the action centerpiece of the film, it still suffers for it in my opinion. Oh and can someone please explain that S & M shoot-out scene? Seemed out of place for me. And no matter how grand the battle royale between Smith and Neo (and it is pretty cool if you ask me), none of their face-offs are nearly as good as the original one. It was more visceral, more real, and just plain better...but that doesn't mean the others suck, either. What used to really irk me about Revolutions was its ending. I used to absolutely hate it, but oddly that completely changed with my recent viewing. It just made more sense to me as I thought about it. I mean, seeing Neo take out the machines and destroy the Matrix would have been awesome, but wow the logistics of all those instantly freed minds are staggering to say the least. His sacrifice for peace is an example we should harken to...sometimes victory is about trying to settle the differences we have with countries and/or cultures rather than attempting to eradicate them from the face of the earth. If I rate this movie on its own, it would not do too well. Rather, I feel its merit (along with Reloaded) rests in being a part of a whole.  Given that, even with its flaws I will go with 4 stars.    The Animatrix is an excellent collection and gets no less than 5 stars from me, as does this collection as a whole. Re-visiting these amazing films with fresh perspective really changed my opinions for the better. This trilogy now has a place of honor along with my favorite series of all time!
